css-1s3qu2q" builder-id="builder-64b1d2c79e1a40a392c2d854cfcc9acf"><div class="builder-custom-code"><p></p>
<center>
  <strong>
    Tom Hegel <br />
    Cloud Security Researcher – Lacework Labs</strong
  >
</center>
<p></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>
  Malicious actors often add SSH keys to victim hosts for persistence (<a
    href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1098/004/"
    rel="noopener"
    target="_blank"
    >T1098.004</a
  >), in this blog we will show you how to hunt with that knowledge. The process
  of identifying malicious activity through threat intelligence pivoting is
  standard practice for any CTI analyst. For readers unfamiliar with the
  process, pivoting is the process of identifying similar or associated elements
  of data – such as network and host-based activity. For example, pivoting can
  be done through network characteristics such as unique C2 server
  configurations. Additionally, the process of pivoting can be applied to other
  areas, such as malware samples designed in similar ways or containing similar
  functionality. The process of pivoting has been written about extensively, and
  I recommend readers review the paper “<a
    href="https://www.domaintools.com/content/formulating-a-robust-pivoting-methodology.pdf"
    target="_blank"
    rel="noopener"
    >Formulating a Robust Pivoting Methodology</a
  >”. Ultimately, we want to pivot to discover new activity based on a
  relationship of many types.
</p>
<h2>Pivot Quality</h2>
<p>
  When it comes to pivoting, it’s important to avoid common mistakes. For
  example, an analyst could incorrectly associate a collection of domains
  through a bad pivot on a shared historical DNS record to a parked IP address.
  On the other hand, a malware sample could be related to others based on code
  reuse or functionality. A common opportunistic threat which we observe related
  to Linux and Cloud services are botnets of illicit cryptocurrency miners (<a
    href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1496/"
    target="_blank"
    rel="noopener"
    >T1496</a
  >). A large majority of these ongoing attacks originate from the delivery and
  execution of scripts. Now, the scripts happen to reuse code from others on a
  large scale. For example, we’ve previously mentioned malicious scripts which
  uninstall monitoring agents for Tencent Host Security (YunJing) and the
  <a href="/platform/threat-detection/">Threat Detection</a> Service (Aegis)
  from Alibaba Cloud.
</p>
<p>
  Lacework has observed this function being reused across various cloud
  targeting threats, such as the H2miner botnet, TeamTNT, and Rocke Group (<a
    href="https://attack.mitre.org/groups/G0106/"
    target="_blank"
    rel="noopener"
    >G0106</a
  >). In fact, it’s almost a challenge to find similar scripts that do not
  contain this general function. In more capable and targeted adversary groups,
  the use of the
  <a
    href="https://nao-sec.org/2020/01/an-overhead-view-of-the-royal-road.html"
    target="_blank"
    rel="noopener"
    >Royal Road</a
  >, or “8.t” toolkit is an example of a potential pivot expanding your
  collection outside of your interests. Royal Road is a shared RTF weaponizer
  that has been observed in use across multiple seemingly unrelated APT groups
  such as Goblin Panda, APT40, and ICEFOG (China)
  <a
    href="https://github.com/malwarekiwi/Public-Content/blob/master/Global%20Perspective%20of%20the%20SideWinder%20APT.pdf"
    target="_blank"
    rel="noopener"
    >and then later with SideWinder</a
  >
  (India), later to be in use by various crimeware operators. If you were to
  pivot on the use of Royal Road alone, many incorrect conclusions could be
  made, which is a reason why attribution should be taken with caution.
  Nonetheless, it is important to challenge what is truly unique to your
  investigation interest and what could be shared widely among different actors.
</p>
<p></p>
<center>
  <img
    decoding="async"
    loading="lazy"
    class="wp-image-7589 size-large aligncenter"
    src="https://cdn.builder.io/api/v1/image/assets%2Ff3229fa9116546a5bb8eacf7cc42baaa%2Fa78804833c6d4b4dad0079e6ae2346a9?format=webp"
    alt=""
    width="640"
    height="351"
  />
</center>
<p></p>
<p style="text-align:center">
  <span style="font-size:10pt"
    >Royal Road Use Timeline, Source:
    <a
      href="https://www.anomali.com/blog/analyzing-digital-quartermasters-in-asia-do-chinese-and-indian-apts-have-a-shared-supply-chain"
      target="_blank"
      rel="noopener"
      >Analyzing Digital Quartermasters in Asia – Anomali</a
    ></span
  >
</p>
<h2>Malicious SSH Keys</h2>
<p>
  Let’s take a look at private SSH keys – a common, often unique, string we can
  observe in great quantities when searching for malware. A recent example of a
  malicious script that is easy to come across are Linux OS Raspberry Pi bots.
</p>
<p>
  The script originally
  <a
    href="https://vms.drweb.com/virus/?_is=1&amp;i=15389228"
    target="_blank"
    rel="noopener"
    >came to light in 2017</a
  >
  with the objective of installing an illicit miner. Today we continue to
  observe a new flood of the current-day version with the addition of many new
  features, including adding it to an IRC botnet, such as sample
  <span style="font-family:'courier new', courier, monospace"
    >65b4e03c1300eebf6823bb6834461960</span
  >. At one point in the script, the actor’s SSH key is added into
  authorized_keys for retaining access if the IRC bot is remediated by the
  victim.
</p>
<p></p>
<center>
  <img
    decoding="async"
    loading="lazy"
    class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-7590"
    src="https://cdn.builder.io/api/v1/image/assets%2Ff3229fa9116546a5bb8eacf7cc42baaa%2F8a56eec2493b4258bac52b72d986c5b6?format=webp"
    alt=""
    width="1600"
    height="270"
  />
</center>
<p></p>
<p style="text-align:center">
  <span style="font-size:10pt">SSH Key Addition Command</span>
</p>
<p>
  In this case, our working theory is the samples containing the same key are
  likely associated with the same operator and/or campaign. An SSH key is an
  interesting pivot because it is not only based on the uniqueness of a specific
  sample, it is also a unique identifier the attacker would have to manage as
  well. This means a pivot to all samples containing this key could be isolated
  to the same access manager/operator or a different actor reusing the code by
  accident (less likely). Of course, we must consider if any automated “builder”
  could include this at creation.
</p>
<h2>Yara Rules</h2>
<p>
  So how do we pivot on this simple string? One can make use of their own sample
  telemetry or database if possible. The use of VirusTotal here can grant us
  access to samples as well. A very basic Yara could be:
</p>
</div></div><div class="builder-block builder-658c482cc8e4468084dafbeb166cefc3 builder-has-component css-1s3qu2q" builder-id="builder-658c482cc8e4468084dafbeb166cefc3"><div class="codeBlock my-5"><div class="sc-guDLey ihVNUz"><span style="font-size:inherit;font-family:inherit;background:#002451;color:white;border-radius:3px;display:inline;line-height:1.4285714285714286;overflow-x:auto;white-space:pre-wrap;padding:2px 4px"><code style="white-space:pre;float:left;padding-right:10px"><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">1
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">2
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">3
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">4
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">5
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">6
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">7
</span></code><code style="white-space:pre">rule pi_irc_botnet {
  strings:
    $a1 = &quot;AAAAB3NzaC1yc2EAAAADAQABAAABAQCl0kIN33IJISIufmqpqg54D6s4J0L7XV2kep0rNzgY1S1IdE8HDef7z1ipBVuGTygGsq+x4yVnxveGshVP48YmicQHJMCIljmn6Po0RMC48qihm/9ytoEYtkKkeiTR02c6DyIcDnX3QdlSmEqPqSNRQ/XDgM7qIB/VpYtAhK/7DoE8pqdoFNBU5+JlqeWYpsMO+qkHugKA5U22wEGs8xG2XyyDtrBcw10xz+M7U8Vpt0tEadeV973tXNNNpUgYGIFEsrDEAjbMkEsUw+iQmXg37EusEFjCVjBySGH3F+EQtwin3YmxbB9HRMzOIzNnXwCFaYU5JjTNnzylUBp/XB6B&quot;
    $a2 = &quot;&gt;&gt; /root/.ssh/authorized_keys&quot;
  condition:
    all of them
}</code></span><button aria-label="Copy Code" type="button" class="sc-beySPh eODZAI"><svg class="icon" viewBox="0 0 384 512" width="16pt" height="16pt" fill="white"><path d="M280 240H168c-4.4 0-8 3.6-8 8v16c0 4.4 3.6 8 8 8h112c4.4 0 8-3.6 8-8v-16c0-4.4-3.6-8-8-8zm0 96H168c-4.4 0-8 3.6-8 8v16c0 4.4 3.6 8 8 8h112c4.4 0 8-3.6 8-8v-16c0-4.4-3.6-8-8-8zM112 232c-13.3 0-24 10.7-24 24s10.7 24 24 24 24-10.7 24-24-10.7-24-24-24zm0 96c-13.3 0-24 10.7-24 24s10.7 24 24 24 24-10.7 24-24-10.7-24-24-24zM336 64h-80c0-35.3-28.7-64-64-64s-64 28.7-64 64H48C21.5 64 0 85.5 0 112v352c0 26.5 21.5 48 48 48h288c26.5 0 48-21.5 48-48V112c0-26.5-21.5-48-48-48zM192 48c8.8 0 16 7.2 16 16s-7.2 16-16 16-16-7.2-16-16 7.2-16 16-16zm144 408c0 4.4-3.6 8-8 8H56c-4.4 0-8-3.6-8-8V120c0-4.4 3.6-8 8-8h40v32c0 8.8 7.2 16 16 16h160c8.8 0 16-7.2 16-16v-32h40c4.4 0 8 3.6 8 8v336z"></path></svg></button></div></div></div><div class="builder-block builder-323e0fc9b94c4dcd87881836aea0332e builder-has-component css-1s3qu2q" builder-id="builder-323e0fc9b94c4dcd87881836aea0332e"><div class="builder-custom-code"><p>
  That rule is based on a single file we observed maliciously dropping an IRC
  bot on a vulnerable Raspberry Pi, which leads us to over 550 samples on
  VirusTotal (IOCs below).
</p>
<p></p>
<center>
  <img
    decoding="async"
    loading="lazy"
    class="size-full wp-image-7591 aligncenter"
    src="https://cdn.builder.io/api/v1/image/assets%2Ff3229fa9116546a5bb8eacf7cc42baaa%2Fa585478b0acb4c3081195d6a039e4a05?format=webp"
    alt=""
    width="413"
    height="93"
  />
</center>
<p></p>
<p style="text-align:center">
  <span style="font-size:10pt">VirusTotal Retro Results</span>
</p>
<p>
  Inspecting our hundreds of triggering files indicated this to be a strong link
  to associated malicious scripts – generally referenced as
  Backdoor:Linux/IRCbot. While this is a very simple and clear example, I hope
  it sheds some light on how to think about such a direct link to pivot on.
</p>
<h2>Technique Pivots</h2>
<p>
  Now let’s consider how we could widen our Yara rule for the purpose of finding
  samples not using this exact key, but the same technique of adding a key to
  authorized_keys. First, we can look for the echo of the key into
  .ssh/authorized keys based on ASCIIi strings. We must now consider legitimate
  files since we’re not isolated to a specific key. Therefore, we should
  implement the rule with strings we could consider highly suspicious to be
  included in the same script.
</p>
</div></div><div class="builder-block builder-688e81bedb0346f58f0b9997cd5fdf62 builder-has-component css-1s3qu2q" builder-id="builder-688e81bedb0346f58f0b9997cd5fdf62"><div class="codeBlock my-5"><div class="sc-guDLey ihVNUz"><span style="font-size:inherit;font-family:inherit;background:#002451;color:white;border-radius:3px;display:inline;line-height:1.4285714285714286;overflow-x:auto;white-space:pre-wrap;padding:2px 4px"><code style="white-space:pre;float:left;padding-right:10px"><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">1
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">2
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">3
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">4
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">5
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">6
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">7
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">8
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">9
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">10
</span><span class="react-syntax-highlighter-line-number">11
</span></code><code style="white-space:pre">rule sus_ssh_add {
  strings:
    $a1 = &quot;echo \&quot;ssh-rsa AAAA&quot;
    $a2 = &quot;&gt;&gt; /root/.ssh/authorized_keys&quot;
    $b1 = &quot;pkill -f&quot;
    $b2 = &quot;killall&quot;
    $b3 = &quot; &gt;&gt; /etc/hosts&quot;
  condition:
    all of ($a*) and
    any of ($b*)
}</code></span><button aria-label="Copy Code" type="button" class="sc-beySPh eODZAI"><svg class="icon" viewBox="0 0 384 512" width="16pt" height="16pt" fill="white"><path d="M280 240H168c-4.4 0-8 3.6-8 8v16c0 4.4 3.6 8 8 8h112c4.4 0 8-3.6 8-8v-16c0-4.4-3.6-8-8-8zm0 96H168c-4.4 0-8 3.6-8 8v16c0 4.4 3.6 8 8 8h112c4.4 0 8-3.6 8-8v-16c0-4.4-3.6-8-8-8zM112 232c-13.3 0-24 10.7-24 24s10.7 24 24 24 24-10.7 24-24-10.7-24-24-24zm0 96c-13.3 0-24 10.7-24 24s10.7 24 24 24 24-10.7 24-24-10.7-24-24-24zM336 64h-80c0-35.3-28.7-64-64-64s-64 28.7-64 64H48C21.5 64 0 85.5 0 112v352c0 26.5 21.5 48 48 48h288c26.5 0 48-21.5 48-48V112c0-26.5-21.5-48-48-48zM192 48c8.8 0 16 7.2 16 16s-7.2 16-16 16-16-7.2-16-16 7.2-16 16-16zm144 408c0 4.4-3.6 8-8 8H56c-4.4 0-8-3.6-8-8V120c0-4.4 3.6-8 8-8h40v32c0 8.8 7.2 16 16 16h160c8.8 0 16-7.2 16-16v-32h40c4.4 0 8 3.6 8 8v336z"></path></svg></button></div></div></div><div class="builder-block builder-c69992aa502f48618c957a5e87ea136f builder-has-component css-1s3qu2q" builder-id="builder-c69992aa502f48618c957a5e87ea136f"><div class="builder-custom-code"><p>
  As you can see, we added the requirements for a few strings typically seen in
  malicious scripts. This includes process killing commands, and modifications
  to etc/hosts. What we’re looking for here is not directly related to our
  original sample, so not a pivot on association, but a pivot on technique. This
  leads us to unassociated files which we can use for further research. When it
  comes to wide rules such as this, we need to be careful to consider how this
  could lead to false positives. My objective is to find the malicious with
  minimal false positives – so further tuning can be made based on the results
  of such a rule.
</p>
<p>
  There are near endless possibilities for pivoting, and I hope this short post
  helps readers understand the value of some types over others. Our use of an
  actor unique and sample family unique string of an SSH key allows us to
  collect a few hundred associated samples. Additionally there are many things
  we can do to pivot on this technique to find other malicious activity in our
  telemetry.
</p>
<p>
  In upcoming blogs we dive into further pivot techniques and make better use of
  the large influx of scripts reusing code and building off each other for
  improved infections. Please
  <a href="https://twitter.com/laceworklabs" target="_blank" rel="noopener"
    >follow us on Twitter</a
  >
  to keep up with our latest research. All IOCs from this blog are also
  <a
    href="https://github.com/lacework/lacework-labs/tree/master/blog"
    target="_blank"
    rel="noopener"
    >on our GitHub</a
  >.
</p>

Code","options":{"code":"\u003cp\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccenter\u003e\n  \u003cstrong\u003e\n    Tom Hegel \u003cbr /\u003e\n    Cloud Security Researcher – Lacework Labs\u003c/strong\n  \u003e\n\u003c/center\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n  Malicious actors often add SSH keys to victim hosts for persistence (\u003ca\n    href=\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1098/004/\"\n    rel=\"noopener\"\n    target=\"_blank\"\n    \u003eT1098.004\u003c/a\n  \u003e), in this blog we will show you how to hunt with that knowledge. The process\n  of identifying malicious activity through threat intelligence pivoting is\n  standard practice for any CTI analyst. For readers unfamiliar with the\n  process, pivoting is the process of identifying similar or associated elements\n  of data – such as network and host-based activity. For example, pivoting can\n  be done through network characteristics such as unique C2 server\n  configurations. Additionally, the process of pivoting can be applied to other\n  areas, such as malware samples designed in similar ways or containing similar\n  functionality. The process of pivoting has been written about extensively, and\n  I recommend readers review the paper “\u003ca\n    href=\"https://www.domaintools.com/content/formulating-a-robust-pivoting-methodology.pdf\"\n    target=\"_blank\"\n    rel=\"noopener\"\n    \u003eFormulating a Robust Pivoting Methodology\u003c/a\n  \u003e”. Ultimately, we want to pivot to discover new activity based on a\n  relationship of many types.\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003ePivot Quality\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n  When it comes to pivoting, it’s important to avoid common mistakes. For\n  example, an analyst could incorrectly associate a collection of domains\n  through a bad pivot on a shared historical DNS record to a parked IP address.\n  On the other hand, a malware sample could be related to others based on code\n  reuse or functionality. A common opportunistic threat which we observe related\n  to Linux and Cloud services are botnets of illicit cryptocurrency miners (\u003ca\n    href=\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1496/\"\n    target=\"_blank\"\n    rel=\"noopener\"\n    \u003eT1496\u003c/a\n  \u003e). A large majority of these ongoing attacks originate from the delivery and\n  execution of scripts. Now, the scripts happen to reuse code from others on a\n  large scale. For example, we’ve previously mentioned malicious scripts which\n  uninstall monitoring agents for Tencent Host Security (YunJing) and the\n  \u003ca href=\"/platform/threat-detection/\"\u003eThreat Detection\u003c/a\u003e Service (Aegis)\n  from Alibaba Cloud.\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n  Lacework has observed this function being reused across various cloud\n  targeting threats, such as the H2miner botnet, TeamTNT, and Rocke Group (\u003ca\n    href=\"https://attack.mitre.org/groups/G0106/\"\n    target=\"_blank\"\n    rel=\"noopener\"\n    \u003eG0106\u003c/a\n  \u003e). In fact, it’s almost a challenge to find similar scripts that do not\n  contain this general function. In more capable and targeted adversary groups,\n  the use of the\n  \u003ca\n    href=\"https://nao-sec.org/2020/01/an-overhead-view-of-the-royal-road.html\"\n    target=\"_blank\"\n    rel=\"noopener\"\n    \u003eRoyal Road\u003c/a\n  \u003e, or “8.t” toolkit is an example of a potential pivot expanding your\n  collection outside of your interests. Royal Road is a shared RTF weaponizer\n  that has been observed in use across multiple seemingly unrelated APT groups\n  such as Goblin Panda, APT40, and ICEFOG (China)\n  \u003ca\n    href=\"https://github.com/malwarekiwi/Public-Content/blob/master/Global%20Perspective%20of%20the%20SideWinder%20APT.pdf\"\n    target=\"_blank\"\n    rel=\"noopener\"\n    \u003eand then later with SideWinder\u003c/a\n  \u003e\n  (India), later to be in use by various crimeware operators. If you were to\n  pivot on the use of Royal Road alone, many incorrect conclusions could be\n  made, which is a reason why attribution should be taken with caution.\n  Nonetheless, it is important to challenge what is truly unique to your\n  investigation interest and what could be shared widely among different actors.\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccenter\u003e\n  \u003cimg\n    decoding=\"async\"\n    loading=\"lazy\"\n    class=\"wp-image-7589 size-large aligncenter\"\n    src=\"https://cdn.builder.io/api/v1/image/assets%2Ff3229fa9116546a5bb8eacf7cc42baaa%2Fa78804833c6d4b4dad0079e6ae2346a9?format=webp\"\n    alt=\"\"\n    width=\"640\"\n    height=\"351\"\n  /\u003e\n\u003c/center\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align:center\"\u003e\n  \u003cspan style=\"font-size:10pt\"\n    \u003eRoyal Road Use Timeline, Source:\n    \u003ca\n      href=\"https://www.anomali.com/blog/analyzing-digital-quartermasters-in-asia-do-chinese-and-indian-apts-have-a-shared-supply-chain\"\n      target=\"_blank\"\n      rel=\"noopener\"\n      \u003eAnalyzing Digital Quartermasters in Asia – Anomali\u003c/a\n    \u003e\u003c/span\n  \u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eMalicious SSH Keys\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n  Let’s take a look at private SSH keys – a common, often unique, string we can\n  observe in great quantities when searching for malware. Today we continue to\n  observe a new flood of the current-day version with the addition of many new\n  features, including adding it to an IRC botnet, such as sample\n  \u003cspan style=\"font-family:'courier new', courier, monospace\"\n    \u003e65b4e03c1300eebf6823bb6834461960\u003c/span\n  \u003e. At one point in the script, the actor’s SSH key is added into\n  authorized_keys for retaining access if the IRC bot is remediated by the\n  victim.\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccenter\u003e\n  \u003cimg\n    decoding=\"async\"\n    loading=\"lazy\"\n    class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-7590\"\n    src=\"https://cdn.builder.io/api/v1/image/assets%2Ff3229fa9116546a5bb8eacf7cc42baaa%2F8a56eec2493b4258bac52b72d986c5b6?format=webp\"\n    alt=\"\"\n    width=\"1600\"\n    height=\"270\"\n  /\u003e\n\u003c/center\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp style=\"text-align:center\"\u003e\n  \u003cspan style=\"font-size:10pt\"\u003eSSH Key Addition Command\u003c/span\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n  In this case, our working theory is the samples containing the same key are\n  likely associated with the same operator and/or campaign. An SSH key is an\n  interesting pivot because it is not only based on the uniqueness of a specific\n  sample, it is also a unique identifier the attacker would have to manage as\n  well. This means a pivot to all samples containing this key could be isolated\n  to the same access manager/operator or a different actor reusing the code by\n  accident (less likely). Of course, we must consider if any automated “builder”\n  could include this at creation.\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eYara Rules\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n  So how do we pivot on this simple string? One can make use of their own sample\n  telemetry or database if possible. The use of VirusTotal here can grant us\n  access to samples as well. This\n  leads us to unassociated files which we can use for further research. When it\n  comes to wide rules such as this, we need to be careful to consider how this\n  could lead to false positives. My objective is to find the malicious with\n  minimal false positives – so further tuning can be made based on the results\n  of such a rule.\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n  There are near endless possibilities for pivoting, and I hope this short post\n  helps readers understand the value of some types over others.
